STATISTICAL APPENDIX

Definitions and Explanatory Notes

The following general notes supplement the footnotes given below the individual tables:

1. In an attempt to bring the material up-to-date provisional figures are included in

some tables.

2. Figures in some tables have been rounded off to the nearest final digit. Hence

there may be a slight discrepancy between the total as shown and the sum of its

components.

3. Differences as compared with previously published figures are due to

subsequent revisions.

4. The following symbols have been used throughout:-

n.a. = not available

â€” = nil

... = negligible

WEIGHTS AND MEASURES

Conversion Factors

British to Metric Units

1 acre = 0.405 hectares (ha)

1 pound (lb) = 0.454 kilogrammes (kg.)

1 long ton (2240 lbs) 1.016 metric tons (Mt. Ton)

1 hundred weight (cwt) 50.802 kg.

1 mile = 1.609 kilometres (km.)

1 long ton mile = 1.635 Mt. Ton km.

1 lb/acre 1.121 kg./ha

1 cwt/acre 125.536 kgVha

1 pint 0.75 litres

1 imperial gallon = 4.55 litres

Metric to British Units

1 hectare = 2.471 acres

1 kilogramme 2.205 lbs

1 Mt. Ton (1,000 kg.) 0.984 long ton

1 metre 3.281 feet

1 kilometre 0.621 mile

1 Mt. Ton kilometre = 0.612 long ton mile

1 litre = 1.76 pints = 0.219 imp. gallons

1 kg./ha 0.892 lb/acre

Paddy/Rice Conversions

1 bushel of paddy (46 lbs) = 20.87 kg.

1 Mt. Ton paddy = 47.92 bushels paddy

0.7 Mt. Ton rice

1 Mt. Ton rice = 68.46 bushels paddy

1.43 Mt. Ton paddy

1 bushel paddy/acre = 51.55 kg. paddy/ha

Notes : A. The Index numbers are calculated on fixed weights based on the numbers employed as at December 31st,1978. The wage rates used in the calculation of Index Numbers are minimum wages for different trades fixed by the Wages Boards. Figures from 1984 have been revised on monthly wages.

B. The Index for Central Government employees has been revised based on the number of employees and total salaries as at 1 st January, 1986. The Index Numbers from January, 1986 have been spliced. The wage rates used in the calculated of Index Numbers are the initial salaries and wages in each respective scale.

(a) The Index refers to wage rates of tea growing and manufacturing, rubber growing and manufacturing, coconut cocoa, cardamoms, and pepper growing trades only.

(b) Including baking, and tile manufacturing, coconut manufacturing, printing, textile, tyre and tube manufacturing, coir mattresses and bristle export, hosiery manufacturing, engineering, garment manufacturing match manufacturing, biscuit and confectionary, tea export and rubber export trades only.

(c) This includes cinema, motor transport and nursing home trades only.

(d) Combined index for workers in agriculture, workers in industry & commerce and services.

(e) A combined index for Non-Executive Officers and Minor Employees.

-4,389.7 (a) Provisional Source ; Central Bank of Sri Lanka The above presentation conforms as far as possible to international practice as followed by the International Monetary Fund, in their Balance of Payments Yearbook and International Financial Statistics. Item 1 Exports are recorded bn f.o.b. valuation; imports on c.i.f. valuation. Item 5 Passage collections by foreign shipping agents and air lines are included in Item 4.1 - Passenger fares. Item 10 Indicates outright grants received in the form of food aid, commodity aid and project aid. Credit entries refer mainly to an increase in liabilities resulting from imports under trade credits. The debit entries in respect of such imports are in the merchandise account and are recorded on an arrivals basis Debit entries

refer to a decline in such liabilities and are recorded at the time of settlement of payment for such imports. The change in capital of the monetary sector is based on local records. The figures shown in the credit column relate to increase in liabilities to the I.M.F. arising from drawings from the I.M.F. Sri Lanka Rupee values reflect changes in the representative rate of the SDR, Figures in the debit column are in respect of repurchases or repayments, made in instalments to the I.M.F., in respect of earlier drawings. Subscriptions to the I.M.F., consequent to successive increases in Sri Lanka's quota are also shown in the debit column.

Note : The Figures in the above table differ from statistics on external assets published in the Annual Reports and Bulletins up to December 1961. The differences that occur are explained more fully in the footnotes that follow.

(a) Figures upto 1959 include War Loan re-lent to U.K. Government, less the part held by the Currency Board, Central Bank. Figures upto 1952 agree with previously published statistics. Any difference thereafter is due to the inclusion of balances due to Sri Lanka under payments agreement with the People's Republic of China. Except for these balances which are recorded according to local records, the basis of valuation throughout is at face value according to records abroad. Figures since 1985 have been revised to include DST's special revolving credit balances.

(b) As previously published i.e. at face value according to records abroad. Includes foreign assets held by the Sterling Loans Sinking Fund. (c) The assets of the Currency Board were transferred to the Central Bank on August 28, 1950. Figures upto 1949 are as previously published. Thereafter, the figures are as published under the International Reserve in the balance sheet of the Central Bank. The International Reserve also includes balances due to Sri Lanka on bilateral clearing accounts other than with the People's Republic of China and includes statutory reserve requirements of commercial banks since January, 1993. (d) All figures are according to local records, and upto 1947 are as previously published. From 1949 they agree with foreign balances of commercial banks. These balances are the sum of foreign currency on hand and balances due from banks abroad and export bills purchased and discounted.

Pursuant to Section 51 of the Monetary Law Act, the Central Bank's holdings of notes and coins are not considered as part of its currency issue. Source: Central Bank of Sn Lanka

Currency notes in the denomination of Rs.20/- was issued from 4th August 1980, Rs.1,000/- from 23rd December 1981, and Rs.500/- from 9th February, 1982. Coins of Rs.10/- denomination was issued from 11th March, 1987. Currency notes of Rs.1, Cts. 50, Cts. 25, Cts.10 are also included. The value of these notes remained unchanged at Rs.3.5 million, Rs.0.4 million, Rs.0.5 million and Rs.0.5 million, respectively from 1974. Includes coins of the denomination of half a cent (Rs. 005), Rs. 100 and Rs. 500. The value of half cents coins has been constant at Rs. 0.09 million, since 1950, while the value of Rs. 100 coins stands at Rs. 1.8 million and the value of Rs. 500 coins stands at Rs. 17.0 million.

Note : The required reserves are computed weekly every Friday on the basis of deposit liabilities as on the Source: Central Bank of Sri Lanka, previous Wednesday. Figures so computed are applicable until next Thursday.

(a) Excludes inter-bank deposits. (b) Commercial banks are required to maintain special reserves under a reserve tranche system against increase in deposits over the level as at 14th November 1984. Reserves in respect of each tranche are as follows. Up to 3%-Nil Over 6% up to 10%-10% of the increase,

Over 3% up to 6%-5% of the increase Over 10%-15% of the increase.

This requirement was terminated with effect from 28th February 1986. (d) With effect from 9th August 1985, the reserves required to be held by the Commercial banks in respect of time and savings deposits were to take the form of rupee deposits in the Central Bank provided that not exceeding one eighth of such reserves may take the form of bonds issued by the Development Finance Corporation of Ceylon (DFCC) and held by such banks as an asset. With effect from 26th February 1988 the reserves required to be held by the Commercial banks were to take the form of rupee deposits in the Central Bank provided that an amount not exceeding one-tenth of its required reserves in respect of the total of time and savings deposits may take the form of bonds issued by the DFCC and held by such banks as an asset.

With effect from 16th September 1968 the amount of reserves that can be maintained in the form of Treasury Bills was increased to 5 per cent of the total deposits. With effect from 11th January 1991 the amount of reserves that can be maintained in the form of treasury bills was withdrawn.

With effect from 24th January 1992, an amount of till cash over and above two per centum of the total of deposits liabilities but not exceeding four per centum may be maintained as a part of required reserves in the from of Sri Lanka currency notes and coins.

(e) Total till cash less the amount of till cash kept as reserves.

(f) Deposits liabilities have been defined to include foreign currency deposits as well for computation of reserves, w.e.f. 4th September 1992.

(c) Under Sections 10c, 93, 94, 96 and 97 of the Monetary Law Act (Chapter 422) Commercial banks are required to maintain reserves with Central Bank and the ratios so maintained shall not be less than 5 per centum or more than 20 per centum in the case of time and savings deposits and shall not be less than 10 per centum or more than 40 per centum in the case of demand deposits and unused balances of overdrafts allowed. Commercial Bank's Advances Rates

Source: Central Bank of Sri Lanka

(a) The weighted average yield rates on Treasury bills in the primary market at end December 1995 for 3 months, 6 months and 12 months were 19.26, 18.91 and 18.97 per cent per annum, respectively. (b) This is the rate at which the Central Bank grants advances to commercial banks for their temporary liquidity purposes. However, after August, 1977 each bank, depending on selected assets items, was allocated a certain quota of such borrowings at the Bank Rate. Borrowings in excess of this quota were subjected to a penal interest rate of 15 per cent per annum. In September, 1979 a graduated scale of penal rates ranging between 15 to 25 per cent was introduced. It was further revised upward to a range between 20 to 30 per cent effective from April, 1980. With effect from August, 1981 the Bank Rate was raised to 14 per cent, while the penal rates were raised to a range between 21 to 30. In March, 1983, the Bank Rate was brought down to 13 per cent and the penal rate was fixed at 20 per cent. With effect from 23rd November, 1983 the practice of granting penal rate advances to commercial banks was discontinued. With effect from 30th December, 1985 the Central Bank reduced the lending rates on its advances to 11 per cent. A further reduction, to 10 per cent, was made with effect from 7th August 1987. The Central Bank increased the lending rate on advances to 14 per cent with effect from 15th June, 1989. The rate was increased again to 15 per cent, with effect from 6th July, 1990 and to 17 per cent with effect from 4th January, 1991.
